- 22:30–02:00 a recurring problem. She cannot sleep at the hour normal people sleep. She uses these hours for the sweep, for writing (see 6.10), for sitting at a window.
- 02:00ish sleep, until the 04:10 waking.

### 6.7 Habits of Place

Rachel claims spaces by sightline and by exit. Surveillance would map her preferred chairs in every room she frequents: always with back to a wall, always with a clear line to a door and a window. In the Excalibur lighthouse her chair was the one in the kitchen alcove facing the door. At the mansion, the window seat in the upstairs library. On the Starjammer, the corner of the mess opposite the galley arch. On Krakoa, an unobtrusive perch on the rocks above a particular tide pool, returned to repeatedly across the Krakoan years.

She walks the perimeter of any new building within the first day of occupancy. This is not stealthy; she will walk it openly, hand trailing the wall, and she has been observed by every cohort she has lived with doing this. The walls are being mapped — telepathically and structurally — for the layout she will need if she has to defend or evacuate the space.

She does not ornament her rooms. The walls stay bare. The exceptions, when they appear, are revealing: a photograph of Nathan as an infant kept face-down on the desk and turned up only at night; a small Askani-script fragment pinned inside a drawer; once, in the *X-Men: Gold* era, a child's drawing given her by a student kept inside a book. The ornaments are private, not displayed.

### 6.8 Routes and Geography

Rachel walks more than she flies or teleports when not on mission. The walking is partly habit and partly because she thinks better in motion and because her relationship with her own power is one of deliberate restraint — she does not want to use the abilities for the small things lest she stop being able to distinguish the small things from the large.

Her walking routes in any environment of residence are repeating loops and surveillance would map three to five. She varies the order but not the components. The walks are at dawn or in the late evening. She acknowledges people she passes with a small nod and does not stop to talk unless addressed by name. In cities she is uncomfortable and the routes shorten and tighten; in wilderness or coastline she lengthens them and they become almost meditative.

She has favored coastlines. Lighthouse-era Muir, the Krakoan tide pool, the Shi'ar gardens with the artificial sea. Water at the edge of a continent is the geography she chooses when she can. Possible read: the Hound camps were inland.

### 6.9 Communication Patterns

She is sparse with words in person and dense with them telepathically. Voice-conversation Rachel speaks in short complete sentences, listens more than she speaks, and answers questions with one sentence fewer than asked. Telepathic Rachel — with intimates, with her brother, in command — is articulate, sometimes funny, lyrical when describing the timestream. The disjunction is striking and consistent: her interior voice is larger than her exterior voice.

She does not initiate small talk. She does respond to it when offered, with apparent effort and improving competence across the decades. She greets people she loves with physical touch — a hand on the upper arm, a brief embrace, a hand to the back of the head with Kurt and Kitty specifically — and people she does not know with a measured nod and her hands visible. The hands-visible habit is Hound-conditioning residue and she does not know she does it.

She does not lie well. She lies by omission constantly and effectively; outright fabrication is something she avoids and performs poorly when forced. Interrogators have noted that pressing her on a direct factual lie produces a flicker — eye-cut down and left, brief tongue-to-incisor — that she cannot suppress.

### 6.10 The Private Hours — What She Does Alone

Between 22:30 and the late sleep, alone, Rachel:

- Writes. Not a journal in the conventional sense. Surveillance would recover loose pages, often Askani-script, often partial, dated obsessively. The content cross-references possible futures she has seen, names she does not want to forget, small notations of the day's interactions that mattered. She destroys these on a rolling cycle, never keeping more than a few weeks' worth. The writing is in Askani because she has correctly assessed that no one alive in the present can read it without effort.
- Runs probability trees. This is internal and unobservable except by its outputs. She models forward branches from current events, checking for the camp-signature. This is the activity that catastrophizes her and that she cannot stop doing.
- Visits Nathan telepathically when he permits it. The exchanges are brief and routine — confirmation of mutual aliveness, small updates — and they matter to her disproportionately. On nights she cannot reach him she sleeps worse.
- Listens to music, rarely, and only old. Folk material from her actual childhood era and earlier, Shi'ar harmonics from her command years, occasional classical. Never current popular music; she finds it disorienting in a way she has not explained.
- Holds the small worn band from her pocket and does not put it on.

### 6.11 Weekly and Monthly Rhythms

Across a week, surveillance would log:

- Two to three nights of poorer sleep than the baseline (three hours or under, with extended sweep activity).
- One deliberate visit to someone she is maintaining attachment to — Jean, Scott, Kurt, Kitty, Nathan when reachable, depending on era and proximity. These visits are scheduled internally though never announced as such; she will arrive when she has decided it has been long enough.
- One occasion of unexpected affect breakthrough — usually brief, usually private, usually triggered by a sensory match (a child's laugh, a particular smell, a Sentinel reference in conversation). Recovery to operational baseline within ten to thirty minutes.
- At least one extended solitary walk of two-plus hours.

Across a month: a quieter day, usually around the same date, which she does not annotate but which surveillance could correlate to the anniversary of an event from her future-past — a family member's death-date in her timeline, the date of her capture, the date she became Mother Askani. She does not commemorate these openly. She works through them, eats less, sleeps worse, and is gentler with the people around her than usual.

### 6.12 What Surveillance Would Miss

The deepest of Rachel's daily activity is not observable from outside. The constant low-grade telepathic monitoring of the people she loves — confirming heartbeats, confirming presence, confirming timeline — runs underneath everything else she does and costs her energy she does not account for. The probability-tree work runs in parallel with conversation, meals, training. The grief is continuous and quiet and does not surface as behavior most days.

What a week of surveillance would correctly conclude: that the subject is disciplined, austere, vigilant to the point of pathology, deeply attached to a small number of named individuals, and organized around the prevention of a catastrophe that has not happened in the timeline she occupies.

What it would miss without the longitudinal file: that every one of these routines is a recovery practice. The perimeter walk, the tattoo touch, the cold water, the bare walls, the small blade, the long shower, the Askani writing, the held-but-unworn band — these are not the habits of a damaged person performing function. They are the habits of a person who has decided, deliberately and repeatedly across forty years of recorded existence, to remain alive and useful in a world that is not the world she was made for. The routine is the recovery. The recovery is the routine. She gets up at 04:30, touches the brand, and does it again.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU DOSSIER — RACHEL ANNE SUMMERS
## Section VI: Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

The subject's family file is, structurally, the densest in her dossier. She is a time-displaced child of a family that, in her native continuity, was murdered before her adolescence and which, in her present continuity, *exists without her in it*. Every relationship documented below is therefore double-tracked: there is the relationship as she remembers it (the dead version), and there is the relationship as she navigates it now (the living, divergent version). She is the only party in any of these dyads who carries both ledgers. This is the master fact of her relational life and must be held in mind as we proceed.

---

### 6.1 — SCOTT SUMMERS (Father / Cyclops)

**The structural problem.** Of all Rachel's family bonds, this is the one most distorted by chronology and the one she has worked hardest, longest, and least successfully to repair. In her native timeline, Scott was father — present, ordinary, dead before she reached adulthood. In the present, Scott is a man roughly her own apparent age, who has never had a daughter, who fathered Nathan with Madelyne Pryor (a Jean-clone whose existence is itself a wound), and who, by the time Rachel was emotionally ready to claim him, had moved into the political-military leadership of the mutant species.

**The approach pattern.** Surveillance before contact. *UXM* #193 establishes the behavior — she is in the trees outside the mansion before she ever knocks on a door. In #201 she watches him in the nursery with infant Nathan and does not announce herself; she leaves a psychic trace he half-registers and dismisses. She does this for *weeks* of in-story time before any direct address. This is the Hound's stalking methodology repurposed as a daughter's reconnaissance: she cannot tolerate being unseen, and she cannot tolerate being seen unprepared, so she watches.

**The disclosure failure.** Scott does not recognize her as his daughter on first encounter, and when he does learn, he does not absorb it as Rachel needs him to absorb it. His affect is muted, his framing is intellectual ("a daughter from a future that won't happen"), and his subsequent behavior toward her remains cordial-but-distanced for years. From Rachel's side this lands as a re-abandonment, but her conditioning prevents her from naming it; she instead converts the wound into operational deference. She will *do for* him what she cannot *be to* him. She fights at his side. She defers to his tactical judgment. She does not call him "Dad" with any regularity until very late in the published record, and when she does it is always at an emotional peak — never casually, never as a relational baseline.

**Evolution.** The dynamic begins to thaw across the late-2000s material and into the Krakoan period. Scott, by then a different man — twice-resurrected himself, having lost and reclaimed his own children — becomes capable of meeting her partway. *Trial of Magneto* and *Rise of the Powers of X* show direct father-daughter scenes where the deferential overlay drops and what remains is two combat veterans who happen to be parent and child. This is the closest she gets to integration with him, and it is hard-won.

**Trust and safety.** She trusts him operationally without reservation. She trusts him emotionally only on a delay and only in private. She has never been physically afraid of him (she has been physically afraid of almost everyone else of comparable power-class at some point). The safety is real; the warmth is partial.

**Failure mode.** When Scott chooses the mission over the family — and he does, repeatedly, by temperament — Rachel does not protest. She *agrees*. This is the failure mode: the daughter who refuses to ever be the cost of the father's distraction, because she has internalized that the mission is what fathers do and what daughters die for. She will silently absorb any neglect framed as duty. Tracking the file: she has never once called him out for it.

**Leverage.** Anyone who threatens Scott unlocks her stage-three escalation. Anyone who claims her ahead of Scott (a mentor, a lover, a god) earns her suspicion. The single phrase most likely to crack her composure is some variant of *"you are not his."*

---

### 6.2 — JEAN GREY (Mother / Phoenix / Marvel Girl)

**The primary wound.** If Scott is the relationship of deferred recognition, Jean is the relationship of *competing identity*. Rachel is genetically, telepathically, and iconographically her mother's daughter. She wears (intermittently) her mother's costume, holds her mother's cosmic bond, and is read by every observer as Jean's continuation. This is both her deepest comfort and her most acute distress, because what Rachel actually wants is to be Jean's *daughter*, not Jean's *echo*, and the world keeps collapsing the distinction.

**Native-timeline Jean.** Dead before Rachel was old enough to consolidate adult memory of her. Rachel's interior Jean is a child's Jean — idealized, soft, the warmth before the world ended. This is a mother she can neither verify nor disappoint, and the imago has remained largely intact across forty years.

**Present-timeline Jean.** A peer-aged woman who, for most of the published record, was either dead, dispersed into the Phoenix, replaced by Madelyne, or otherwise unavailable. The two of them have had remarkably few sustained on-page scenes given the centrality of the bond. When they do interact (notably *X-Men: Red*, the Krakoan-era material, *Rise of the Powers of X*), the dynamic is striking: Jean treats Rachel with a tenderness that is unmistakably maternal in register but also faintly bewildered, as if Jean is feeling for a script she does not have. Rachel reciprocates with a deference far in excess of the chronological age gap — she becomes younger in Jean's presence, her voice softens, her telepathic signature dampens, her stance opens.

**The Phoenix overlap.** Phoenix is the third party in this dyad and behaves like a shared inheritance no one knows how to divide. Early Rachel claims it as her birthright (*UXM* #199, the Holocaust Memorial scene). Later Rachel learns to hold it provisionally — not as a claim against Jean, but as a relay. This is real psychological work. By the late record she has stopped competing for the Phoenix bond and started treating it as something the two of them share with neither in primacy. This is one of her most genuine integrations.

**Trust and safety.** She trusts Jean absolutely on the imago level and provisionally on the operational level. The provisional caveat is because Jean has the unique capacity to *see her completely* — Jean is the one telepath Rachel cannot screen from — and this means Jean knows the Hound years in raw form. Rachel has never been comfortable with this knowledge sitting in another person, even (especially) her mother.

**Failure mode.** She regresses in Jean's presence. The capable Prestige-era leader becomes, in extended Jean-contact, a much younger version of herself. This is not pathological per se but it is operationally noticeable and would be exploitable by any actor able to insert themselves between them.

**Leverage.** The most dangerous leverage point in the entire file: convince Rachel that Jean does not want her, or that Rachel's existence diminishes Jean. Either claim collapses the imago, and the imago is load-bearing for everything else.

---

### 6.3 — NATHAN CHRISTOPHER SUMMERS (Brother / Son / Cable)

**The category problem.** Nathan is Rachel's most chronologically deranged relationship and, paradoxically, her most stable one. In her native timeline he was never born. In the present timeline he is her infant half-brother. In the Askani timeline she raised him from toddlerhood to adolescence as Mother Askani and is, functionally, his mother. In the present-present he is a grizzled militarized adult older in apparent age than she is. All four of these relationships exist simultaneously in her head and she navigates them, as noted in the personality workup, *without apparent strain.* This is remarkable. It is also the single best evidence in the file for her late-career integration: she is fluent in a kind of relational topology that breaks most people.

**The infant scene.** *UXM* #201, the nursery. She holds the baby Nathan and weeps. This is the cleanest emotional moment in early Rachel's entire record. The baby is the brother she never had, the family she could not save, the proof that there is something in this timeline worth defending. She bonds to him in seconds and the bond has never broken since. Every subsequent Nathan-relationship overlays on this primary imprint.

**The Askani period.** She is dying when she pulls him into the future. She raises him in a war-torn theocracy that she herself founded. She teaches him to hold the Askani gestalt, to channel telepathic discipline, to survive the Techno-Organic virus through sustained psionic containment. She loses him repeatedly in the chaos. She dies, in the operative sense, before he reaches full adulthood. This is the maternal experience she was denied in every other context, and she did it under terminal-illness conditions, knowing she would not see the outcome. The Askani period is the single most psychologically transformative episode of her life and Nathan is its center.

**The adult Cable relationship.** When she returns to the present and encounters Cable as the grown man she raised as a child, her behavior is striking for its lack of disorientation. She accepts the topological scramble immediately. She refers to him alternately as brother and as son depending on context, and the context-switching is fluid. Cable, for his part, treats her with a deference no one else receives — not even Scott. He calls her by the Askani honorific in private. He defers to her tactical reads. He physically positions himself to her flank in combat, a tell he does not show with other family.

**Trust and safety.** Total, mutually, across all four relationship-categories. This is the only family bond in her file with zero asterisks.

**Failure mode.** She will sacrifice essentially anything to protect him. She has done so. She will do so again. The failure mode is therefore that he is the lever any sufficiently informed adversary can use to move her — and *he knows it*, which is part of why he keeps his distance operationally.

**Leverage.** Threaten Nathan and she escalates past stage three without intermediate steps. Threaten Nathan as a child specifically — in any time period — and she will exit the timeline to prevent it.

---

### 6.4 — NATE GREY (Brother-Adjacent / X-Man)

**Status.** The genetic brother from a third timeline (the Age of Apocalypse engineering of Sinister), arrived in the present without her shared history. The relationship is real but thin. She acknowledges the bond; she does not lean on it. He occupies a sibling-shaped slot that she keeps cordial and at distance. There is some evidence she finds him distressing — he is what she might have been if her Phoenix-vector had been somatic rather than cosmic, and the parallel is uncomfortable. She does not avoid him; she does not seek him. The dossier flag: *low investment, high potential volatility if activated.*

---

### 6.5 — MADELYNE PRYOR (The Clone / Goblin Queen / Stepmother-Figure)

**The taxonomic problem.** Madelyne is genetically Rachel's mother (Jean-clone) and biographically Nathan's mother. She is therefore something for which English lacks a word — Rachel's mother and not her mother, her father's wife and not her parent, the woman who bore the brother Rachel mothered. Rachel's response to this taxonomic impossibility is, characteristically, to refuse the category entirely and treat Madelyne as a discrete person.

**The dynamic.** Volatile. Madelyne, particularly in her Goblin Queen and later antagonist phases, weaponizes the genetic overlap to wound Rachel — she has more than once attempted to assert maternal claim over Rachel as a manipulation tactic. Rachel's defense is unusually clean: she does not engage the claim at all. She fights Madelyne as an adversary when required, mourns her as a relative when appropriate, and does not allow the Jean-overlap to confuse her targeting. This is one of the relationships in which Rachel's late-career discipline is most visible.

**Trust and safety.** None and none. Rachel keeps Madelyne in the threat ledger by default and adjusts upward as events warrant.

**Failure mode.** Madelyne knows where the Jean-wound is. If she presses it skillfully enough (rather than crudely, which is her tendency), she could theoretically destabilize Rachel. To date, her hostility has been too overt for the leverage to land.

**Leverage.** Any Madelyne-action that implicates Nathan flips Rachel from cautious to lethal. Any Madelyne-action that mimics Jean's voice or affect specifically (which Madelyne is *capable* of doing) is the more dangerous attack vector and one Rachel has not fully hardened against.

---

### 6.6 — ALEX SUMMERS (Uncle / Havok) and GABRIEL SUMMERS (Uncle / Vulcan)

**Alex.** Acknowledged uncle, low contact. The bond is cordial, occasionally warm in shared-mission contexts, never primary. Rachel treats Alex with a wary respect — he is a Summers, he reads as family, he has also been unstable enough across the record (Goblin Queen-era possession, Dark Avengers period) to remain in a half-trust bracket. She does not confide in him. She would fight for him.

**Gabriel.** A different file entirely. Vulcan registers in Rachel's threat ledger as family-who-is-enemy, a category that maps directly onto her Hound-era experience of *being family-who-was-enemy*. There is, beneath the operational hostility, a recognizable identification — she knows what it is to be a Summers who was broken by captivity and weaponized against kin. She does not voice this. She has not, to date, attempted reclamation. The dossier flag: *if Vulcan were ever genuinely reachable, she is the family member most likely to try.*

---

### 6.7 — THE DEAD FAMILY (Native-Timeline Scott, Native-Timeline Jean, the Unborn Siblings, Franklin Richards as he existed in her future)

**The structural fact.** Rachel grieves a family that, in this timeline, never died — because in this timeline they never lived the lives she remembers. This is the prolonged-grief substrate noted in the psychiatric workup, and it deserves its own subsection because she treats the native-timeline dead as ongoing relationships, not as memories.

**The ritual.** There is evidence across the record (Excalibur run, Askani-era flashbacks, late solo moments in *X-Men: Gold*) that Rachel maintains an interior practice of *addressing* the native-timeline dead. She speaks to them. She reports to them. She asks them for guidance. This is not psychotic — she does not believe she is receiving answers — it is closer to a religious practice, and it predates and underlies the Askani faith she later codified. The Askani religion is, in its deep structure, Rachel's private mourning rite scaled into a theology.

**The native Scott.** Remembered as warmer, more present, more domestic than the present-day Scott. Rachel does not say this aloud and may not fully admit it to herself, but the comparison is operative: every interaction with present-Scott is silently scored against the remembered original, and present-Scott consistently underperforms. This is not fair to present-Scott and Rachel knows it; she manages the unfairness by suppression.

**The native Jean.** Remembered with a child's mythic clarity. The imago is unfalsifiable. Present-Jean is held to no comparison because present-Jean *is* the imago insofar as Rachel can tolerate the conflation.

**The unborn siblings.** There is canonical implication (varying across writers) that Rachel's native family included siblings who did not survive even into her own memory clearly. These appear in dream-sequence material and in fragmentary Askani flashbacks. She does not name them often. They function in her interior life as a generalized population of *the family I could not save*, and they are part of why she imprints so violently on any child in need of protection.

**Franklin Richards as native-future figure.** In her timeline, Franklin was her adolescent companion and possibly more — the canon is deliberate in its ambiguity. He is the one peer attachment in her dead-family file, and he is the template for every subsequent romantic possibility. The present-day Franklin (a child, then a reality-warper, never her peer in lived time) cannot meet the template, and she has had the discipline not to demand it of him. But the unfulfilled adolescent bond is part of why her adult romantic life is sparse: the original was lost before consummation and the comparison set is short.

**Trust and safety.** Total trust in the dead, because the dead cannot revise. This is the seductive feature of grief-as-relationship and Rachel knows it; she has spoken (Askani material) about the danger of preferring the dead because they are *fixed*. That she names this danger is evidence she has examined it.

**Failure mode.** When present-family disappoints, she retreats to the dead. The retreat is invisible to observers because it is wholly interior, but it is operative: she will be physically present in a room with her living parents and emotionally elsewhere with the dead ones. The frequency of this retreat has decreased markedly across the late record.

**Leverage.** Any actor capable of impersonating or simulating the native-timeline dead has catastrophic leverage. She has been targeted on this vector before (Mojo, illusionists, psychic adversaries) and her defenses are surprisingly weak here — she *wants* to believe, and the wanting compromises her screening. This is the single most exploitable seam in her family file and it should be flagged in red.

---

### 6.8 — STRUCTURAL NOTE ON THE FAMILY FILE AS A WHOLE

Rachel Summers is the only X-character whose family relationships must be diagrammed in four dimensions rather than two. The standard genealogical chart fails on contact with her: she is older than her brother and younger than her brother, daughter to a mother her own apparent age, mother to a man who outranks her in years, mourner of relatives who are alive in front of her, sister-by-genetics to a man from a third timeline whose existence she finds unsettling, antagonist-and-kin to a clone of her own mother. That she functions inside this topology at all is remarkable. That she has, across forty years of record, *integrated* it into a stable operational identity — Prestige, Mother Askani, Phoenix-bearer, daughter, sister, mother, mourner, all at once — is the single strongest piece of evidence for the prognosis offered in the personality workup.

The family is where she was destroyed. It is also where she has done her most successful repair work. The wounds remain palpable in every encounter; the function, against every reasonable expectation, holds.

— *UATU, watching.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

A working principle before the entries: Rachel Summers's relational economy operates on a tier system that her conscious mind does not acknowledge but her behavior consistently exposes. Tier zero is *kin-in-the-blood* — Scott, Jean, Nathan, Stephen — covered in the Family file. Tier one, addressed here, is **chosen family**: the small group she has decided, with the full force of her unconscious, are non-negotiable. Tier two is **trusted teammates** with whom she will share tactical risk but not interiority. Tier three is **functional allies** — people whose competence she respects and whose welfare she will defend, but whom she does not let inside. The tiers are sticky: once she has slotted someone, she rarely re-slots them, and a betrayal does not demote so much as it shatters. There are very few names in tier one. The file's central observation is that the names in that tier are almost all women who showed her, in the first thirty days of contact, that she could collapse without being collected, weaponized, or sent away.

---

## 1. Katherine "Kate / Kitty" Pryde — Anchor, Co-Conspirator, Mirror-Self

The single most load-bearing relationship in Rachel's life outside her parents, and arguably more functionally important than either, because Kate is the only person who knew her *before*. The bond is forged in *Uncanny X-Men* #141: the older Kate Pryde of the Days of Future Past timeline is the consciousness Rachel psi-links across decades, and the experience of that linkage — two minds braided through time on the same suicidal hope — is the founding act of Rachel's adult interior life. Whatever residue of trust she carries forward from the camps, Kate is the imprint matrix for it.

**History.** In the future timeline they are camp-adjacent: Kate is one of the last free X-Men, Rachel is a hound who has chosen to betray her conditioners. Their first cooperation is treason on both sides. In the present (post-*UXM* #184), the relationship inverts strangely — Kate is now the younger one, a teenager, while Rachel is the older, broken arrival. Neither party manages the inversion gracefully at first; Rachel is jealous of the un-traumatized Kate she encounters, and Kate is unnerved by being recognized as someone she has not yet been. The friendship rebuilds across Excalibur (1988 onward), where the age-gap closes through shared exile in Britain, and then re-stabilizes across every subsequent shared run — *X-Treme X-Men*, *X-Men: Gold* (where Kate as Kitty Pryde captains a team that Rachel re-joins as Prestige), the Krakoan era, and onward.

**Dynamics.** Kate is the only person Rachel will *initiate physical contact* with without first scanning. The behavioral marker is consistent across decades: when Rachel enters a room and Kate is present, Rachel's first orienting movement is to Kate's location, frequently with a hand at the small of Kate's back or a quick shoulder-bump in passing — a touch-confirmation ritual that no other relationship in the file receives. They share a private idiom of gallows humor about the future that excludes everyone else in any room; observers (Storm, Kurt, even Scott) have noted the conversational shorthand as alienating. Kate is also the only person Rachel reliably allows to interrupt her telepathic concentration without being thrown across the room.

**Evolution.** The relationship has moved from co-traumatized adolescents to co-commanders. By the *X-Men: Gold* run, Kate is the captain and Rachel deputies for her without status-friction — a notable absence, because Rachel's hierarchy reflex around all other authority is suspicion. The submission to Kate's command is unique and rests on a foundation Rachel has never re-examined: in the camp-timeline, Kate was older, and Rachel followed her. That somatic hierarchy persists regardless of present-tense age math.

**Trust / safety.** Maximum. Kate is the person Rachel goes to when she is about to dissociate and knows it. The pattern is documentable: late-night arrivals in Kate's quarters, no conversation, sitting on the floor against the bedframe, Kate reading or working while Rachel breathes down. No telepathic spillover incidents during these visits, which itself is diagnostic — Rachel can stand down her surveillance only in two or three identified contexts, and this is the primary one.

**Failure mode.** Suffocating co-dependence under pressure. When both are stressed, they fuse — they will exclude even their other primaries (Kate excluding Lockheed and Peter; Rachel excluding Nathan and her parents). The fusion produces excellent tactical output and degraded psychological hygiene. A separate failure mode: Kate's death or apparent death (multiple instances across the run) is the single most predictable trigger for Rachel's stage-three Phoenix-flare. Selene learned this; the Shi'ar Death Commandos learned this; the file predicts anyone who learns it will not survive learning it twice.

**Leverage.** Threatening Kate is operationally equivalent to threatening Rachel's children. There is no graduated response. This is the largest single vulnerability in Rachel's threat profile.

---

## 2. Ororo Munroe — Storm — First Corrective Mother, Standing Authority

If Kate is the imprint of peer-trust, Storm is the imprint of safe authority — the first adult in the present-tense who did not flinch from Rachel's intensity, did not pity her, and did not try to manage her. *Uncanny X-Men* #198 (the deer-hunt issue) is the foundational text: Rachel, in a feral rage, intends to kill a poacher; Storm, depowered, walks unarmed into striking distance and refuses to step aside. Rachel stands down not because she is overpowered but because Storm offers her, perhaps for the first time in her life, an authority figure who will neither punish nor abandon her for the rage itself.

**History.** Storm is leader of the X-Men when Rachel arrives. She houses her, codes her into the team without forcing trial-by-mission, and absorbs the brunt of Rachel's early displaced grief-aggression. Across Claremont's run Storm functions as primary handler — the person to whom Professor X and Magneto (during his teaching tenure) defer on Rachel-related decisions. Storm gives Rachel the Hound-tattoo headband during a period of public appearances, and later gently insists she stop hiding it. Both gestures are noted by Rachel and remembered for decades.

**Dynamics.** Rachel's body language around Storm is unique in the file: she stands slightly *behind and to the left*, a guardian's position, never a child's. This is a tell. The role Rachel has assigned Storm is *queen*, and herself *the queen's blade*. It is a feudal arrangement Rachel constructed unconsciously because feudal authority is the only authority-template her conditioning prepared her to obey without trauma response — top-down loyalty was the Hound's training, and Storm is the first person Rachel met to whom such loyalty could be offered cleanly.

**Evolution.** Across forty years, the most stable relationship in Rachel's life apart from Kate's. Storm has buried Rachel, mourned Rachel, retrieved Rachel from the timestream, and given Rachel command authority on Storm's own teams. They have never had a sustained falling-out. The only friction-points are when Storm exercises moral authority Rachel disagrees with (the Krakoan amnesty for former Hellfire actors, briefly); Rachel's pattern is to absent herself rather than confront, returning when the disagreement has aged out.

**Trust / safety.** Total in the sense of physical safety and command-trust. Partial in the sense of interiority: Rachel does not collapse in front of Storm. She refuses the role of *cared-for child* with Storm specifically, because to take it would invalidate the *blade* role she has chosen. This is one of the relationship's costs — Rachel will die for Storm but will not weep in front of her.

**Failure mode.** Over-formal. The relationship can become brittle in long stretches of peace, when there is no enemy for Rachel to lay at Storm's feet. Rachel does not know how to be Storm's friend, only her knight.

**Leverage.** Threat to Storm produces the kill-response, but with cleaner aim than the Kate-threat — no Phoenix-flare, no cosmic-register escalation, just precise lethal force. The difference is diagnostic: Kate is loved as self; Storm is loved as sovereign.

---

## 3. Kurt Wagner — Nightcrawler — Brother of Choice, Confessor

Kurt is the only male peer in Rachel's life who occupies tier one, and the only person across the entire run to whom Rachel speaks unguardedly about spiritual or metaphysical questions. The relationship begins in *Uncanny X-Men* #193 era and deepens in Excalibur, where four years of shared housing in Muir Isle's lighthouse and the Braddock estate forge something neither of them ever quite named.

**History.** Kurt is the X-Man who, on first meeting, did not react to her face. Multiple later panels reference this — Rachel remembers it. He treated her tattoo as he treated his own appearance: a fact of the body, not an event. This single non-response, in a life full of staring, bought him permanent standing.

**Dynamics.** They argue theology. Rachel, with no inherited faith and a personal eschatology forged in extermination camps, is the harder interlocutor; Kurt, devout, does not patronize or flinch. Their conversations across Excalibur (the lighthouse kitchen scenes especially) are the only place in the record Rachel articulates her experience of the Phoenix in religious rather than tactical language. Kurt is also her teleport-transport of first preference: in combat she will fall into his arms without looking, trusting the bamf rotation, which from a hyper-vigilant trauma survivor is a substantial trust gesture.

**Evolution.** Steady. The relationship survives Rachel's exile to the future and her returns; Kurt updates his behavior toward her based on her current age-state without making the updates explicit, a courtesy she registers. Post-Krakoa, with Kurt's deepening role in mutant spiritual life, Rachel quietly defers to him on questions of resurrection ethics — a deference she gives almost no one.

**Trust / safety.** High. Rachel will allow Kurt to read her face, which she does not allow most. She will accept his physical touch (a hand on the shoulder, the carry-teleport hold) without flinch.

**Failure mode.** Romantic ambiguity. There has been, at intervals, a low-grade affection-current that neither acted on, and Rachel manages it by displacing into formal politeness when the ambiguity surfaces. The cost is occasional emotional distance that confuses Kurt and that Rachel will not explain.

**Leverage.** Moderate-to-high. Threat to Kurt produces cold escalation rather than flare; she will hunt the attacker patiently. Kurt is also the only person whose moral disapproval, if delivered seriously and not as performance, will actually stop Rachel in mid-action. He has used this perhaps three times in the record. It worked each time.

---

## 4. Elisabeth Braddock — Betsy / Psylocke — Telepathic Sister, Comparative Case

A more complicated bond, mapped across the Excalibur years and recurring afterward. Betsy is the only other high-grade telepath of Rachel's generation to share housing with her for any extended period, and the relationship is structured by both the rare relief of telepathic peer-presence and the constant friction of two such peers in close quarters.

**History.** They overlap on the Excalibur roster and again at intervals through the X-runs of the 2000s and the Krakoan era. Their early relationship is wary — Betsy's hauteur reads to Rachel as performance, and Rachel's intensity reads to Betsy as adolescent. They warm slowly via shared telepathic etiquette: each is the first peer the other has had who already knows the rules (no skim without invite, no broadcasting in shared space, no parking memory loads in the other's psi-buffer).

**Dynamics.** Sister-rivalry, of the productive kind. They train together — Betsy's psi-blade discipline informs Rachel's later willingness to formalize her own telepathic combat techniques — and they critique each other's tactics with a frankness Rachel accepts from almost no one. The relationship is also one of mutual diagnosis: Betsy is one of the few people who has named Rachel's dissociative compartmentalization out loud and to her face, and the only one to have survived doing so without rupture.

**Evolution.** From rivals to comparable veterans. The body-swap era of Betsy's history complicates Rachel's relationship to her — Rachel has multiple identity-states herself and is unusually able to track Betsy across them, which Betsy has noticed and quietly valued. By the late record, they treat each other as the only person in the room who fully gets it.

**Trust / safety.** Medium-high tactical, medium interiority. Rachel will not collapse in front of Betsy, but she will *think* in front of her — open processing in a shared psi-field, which is its own kind of nakedness.

**Failure mode.** Competitive flare. Under combat stress they can both reach for the same kill-shot and clash. The failure is not catastrophic but is reliable; they have learned to call it and stand down.

**Leverage.** Modest. Threat to Betsy will produce tactical response; it will not destabilize Rachel.

---

## 5. Meggan Puceanu — The Excalibur Sister, Empathic Counterweight

An underrated bond in the record. Meggan, empath and shapeshifter, lived alongside Rachel through the lighthouse years and provided something none of the others did: an *empathic-field stabilizer* against whom Rachel's psi-broadcasts could be safely absorbed without injury.

**History.** Meggan was already on the Excalibur roster when Rachel arrived. The two cohabited for years. Meggan's empathic gift means she could perceive Rachel's interior weather without telepathic intrusion, which Rachel registered as a profound mercy.

**Dynamics.** Domestic. The relationship has the texture of housemates who share tea, share laundry, share grief over the kitchen table. Meggan's tactility (she touches everyone) was the slowest of the Excalibur acclimations for Rachel and ultimately the most regulating: Rachel learned, through Meggan, to register casual touch as non-threat.

**Trust / safety.** Higher than the file initially suggests. Meggan is one of the few people in front of whom Rachel has cried in the record.

**Failure mode.** Distance. Meggan's life took her into different orbits; Rachel does not maintain long-distance friendship well, and the bond has gone dormant for long stretches without rupture. Re-activation on contact is reliable.

**Leverage.** Low strategic, high sentimental. An attacker who targeted Meggan would not be hunted as efficiently as one who targeted Kate, but would be hunted.

---

## 6. Brian Braddock — Captain Britain — Quiet Patriarch of the Lighthouse

Brian's place in the file is structural rather than emotionally central. He provided the *house* — Braddock Manor, the lighthouse — and a model of competent, non-intrusive male authority that Rachel had no prior template for.

**Dynamics.** Cordial and respectful. They did not become intimate. Brian's reliability — his showing up, his paying the bills (literally), his keeping the lights on for a household of exiles — registered with Rachel as a category of care she had not known existed: *infrastructure as love*. She has been quietly grateful for two decades.

**Failure mode.** None active. The relationship is too low-temperature to fail.

**Leverage.** Low-direct, high-via-Meggan.

---

## 7. Franklin Richards — First Romantic Imprint, Tier-Adjacent Kin

Adolescent affection, *Fantastic Four / X-Men* crossover material and *X-Men: The End*. Franklin's standing in the file is double — he is one of the few peers who matches her cosmic register, and he is the boy on whom Rachel learned that she could be wanted.

**History.** Childhood proximity in the camp-timeline (the future Franklin and future Rachel were among the last children alive). In the present-tense their relationship is more diffuse but the imprint persists.

**Dynamics.** Romantic in the soft-imprint sense rather than the consummated sense. They share a precognitive intimacy — both children of impossible parents, both reality-tier mutants, both burdened with futures.

**Failure mode.** Sublimation. Rachel routes Franklin-feelings through her protective drive rather than her romantic life; he becomes one of the children she defends rather than a peer she pursues.

**Leverage.** Threat to Franklin pulls a maternal response disproportionate to his actual age in any given scene.

---

## 8. Korvus Rook'shir — Adult Romantic Partner, Recognition Match

The clearest non-traumatic romance in the record — *Uncanny X-Men* (2008 era, the Aaron / Brubaker / Fraction Shi'ar arcs). Korvus arrives carrying a fragment of the Phoenix in his Blade of the Phoenix; the resonance with Rachel is immediate and mutual, and the relationship begins without the prolonged Rachel-suspicion phase her other intimacies required.

**History.** They meet aboard Shi'ar space, bond in combat, become lovers, separate when arcs send them to different orbits. The relationship is not depicted as ending in trauma — it simply runs out of shared geography.

**Dynamics.** Rachel does not have to perform normality with him; he is also a warrior shaped by cosmic forces, and the absence of explanation between them is itself the gift. They speak about the Phoenix as a colleague speaks about a colleague.

**Trust / safety.** Surprisingly high for the duration. Rachel sleeps in his presence, which is a major behavioral marker.

**Failure mode.** Conflict-avoidance and drift. Neither pursues the other across distance; the relationship dies of starvation rather than betrayal.

**Leverage.** Latent rather than active. Were Korvus to reappear in jeopardy, the response would be intense; in his absence, the file is dormant.

---

## 9. Nathan Christopher Summers — Cable — Brother, Son, Charge

Properly a kin entry, but his standing in Rachel's chosen-family circuitry is so unusual it bears noting here as well. Rachel raised infant Nathan as Mother Askani two thousand years downstream. In the present she is also his sister and is *younger than him* in subjective lived years across most scenes. She manages this without strain because temporal-relational paradox is her native medium, but the relationship is unique in the file: she is the only person in Marvel publishing who is simultaneously someone's mother-figure, sister, and junior.

**Dynamics.** Quiet. They do not perform their history in public. The behavioral marker is private: Rachel will accept Cable's authority on tactical matters where she will accept no one else's, because in the Askani period he was the boy she trained to lead, and her muscle memory respects what she built. Cable, in turn, defers to Rachel on questions of the timestream and on Phoenix matters, because she taught him what he knows.

**Failure mode.** Asymmetric expectation. Rachel sometimes expects Cable to remember things only the older versions of him lived through, and is briefly wounded when he cannot.

**Leverage.** Severe. Cable is non-negotiable.

---

## 10. Charles Xavier — Compromised Mentor, Cautious Distance

Not chosen family. Included here because the question of his mentorship status must be answered: *he was not, and is not, her mentor in the functional sense.* On arrival Rachel sought from him the recognition and direction a younger Jean Grey received, and Charles — already split between the Shadow King recovery era, the X-Men's transition, and his own moral compromises — could not or did not extend it. The wound is permanent and quiet. Rachel works alongside him; she does not trust him with interiority. Post-Krakoan Charles, with the Five and the Quiet Council revelations, has further depleted Rachel's residual respect. She is one of the X-Men least mystified by Xavier, and the file assesses this as accurate clarity rather than bitterness.

**Failure mode.** She will not warn him if she sees him heading off a cliff. She has run out of that energy.

**Leverage.** None. He cannot move her.

---

## 11. Erik Lehnsherr — Magneto — Functional Authority Without Affection

A surprisingly clean working relationship across the New Mutants / X-Men tenure and again on Krakoa. Rachel respects Magneto's clarity about extermination — he was in the camps too, in a different century, and the shorthand between them is real. She will take orders from him. She does not love him. She would not mourn him excessively.

**Failure mode.** If Magneto goes maximalist, Rachel will oppose him without hesitation and without the grief that opposing Xavier would carry. The cleanness is itself diagnostic of the lower attachment.

**Leverage.** None.

---

## 12. Blaire / Dazzler, Logan / Wolverine, Piotr / Colossus — Trusted Teammates, Tier Two

Grouped for economy. Each of these is a long-term colleague Rachel will share risk with and defend, and none of them have been admitted to interiority.

- **Logan:** mutual respect built on shared veteran-of-bad-futures status. He is one of the few who can stand near her in Phoenix-state without being incinerated, partly because of his own resistance and partly because she trusts his lethal competence enough to relax in it. They do not talk about feelings. They drink in silence well. Failure mode: when Logan dies (he has), the loss is real but contained; she does not flare.
- **Dazzler:** sisterly affection from the early Outback / Australian era. Alison is one of the few people who can make Rachel laugh on purpose. The relationship is undemanding and warm. Failure mode: distance; they go years without contact.
- **Colossus:** through Kate-by-extension. The brotherly respect is real; Rachel will not allow Peter to be harmed because Kate would not survive it.

---

## 13. The Askani Sisterhood — Chosen Family in the Downstream

Two thousand years from now, Rachel built a religious order from scratch — the Sisterhood of the Askani — and led it as Mother Askani for decades. The members of this order are her chosen family on a scale unmatched anywhere else in her life, and the loss of them, repeatedly, on her return to the present, is one of the under-mourned griefs in the file. She rarely speaks of them. The few names that surface in the *Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix* and *Cable* materials — Sister Aliya, Madame Sanctity (a complicated case, ultimately adversarial), the unnamed sisters of the New Canaanite resistance — represent decades of accumulated bonds wholesale severed by her return upstream. The behavioral residue: a private liturgical vocabulary she will occasionally murmur under stress, a kneeling posture she falls into when meditating alone, a hand-sign for the Askani'son that she has been observed making absently in moments of relief. She does not explain these. No one asks.

**Failure mode.** This entire community is grief-archived rather than active. The danger is that an Askani-coded threat (Madame Sanctity, surviving New Canaanites) can activate the


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# UATU DOSSIER — RACHEL ANNE SUMMERS
## Section 7: RELATIONSHIPS III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*Cross-reference: Relationships I (Family of Origin), Relationships II (Found Family & Peer Bonds). This section maps the agonistic register — those whose presence in her life is structured by combat, predation, or erotic/intimate vulnerability — and treats the three categories together because, for this subject, the categorical walls are unusually porous. People who have hurt her body have shaped her interior architecture as deeply as people who have shared her bed; in several cases the same person occupies more than one column.*

---

### I. ENEMIES — THE PRIMARY ANTAGONISTS

#### A. Ahab (Roderick Campbell)

**The defining enemy. The one face her file would flag at every airport, every dream, every diagnostic intake.**

*History.* Ahab is the architect of the Hound program, the man who supervised the branding of her face, the conditioning of her psi-systems against her own kin, and the breaking of whatever fourteen-year-old self existed before. First seen properly in *Fantastic Four Annual* #23 and the *Days of Future Present* crossover (1990); his presence haunts her record from her first appearance in *UXM* #141 forward, even before he is given a name and silhouette. He is the proximate cause of every diagnostic line in Section 4 of this file.

*Dynamic.* Persecutor / handler / parental-substitute-by-coercion. The relationship is technically a kind of horrific apprenticeship — he made her what she operationally is (tracker, hunter, weapon) and she has been engaged for forty years in the work of repudiating him without ceasing to use his tools. The recursion is unresolvable: every time she scans a crowd for threat using the hunter's eye, she is using his curriculum. He owns a layer of her cognition she cannot delete.

*Evolution.* From inescapable god-figure (Hound era) → absent specter (1981–1990) → returnable monster (*Days of Future Present*, where she confronts him as an adult and a Phoenix-bearer) → recurring nightmare reactivation across the Krakoan era. Each re-encounter is a re-litigation; she does not "win" them in the standard narrative sense because the victory she requires — undoing the conditioning at its source — is metaphysically unavailable.

*Trust/safety.* Zero. Anti-trust. His mere documented existence in a current timeline registers as an environmental hazard equivalent to airborne neurotoxin.

*Failure mode.* In proximity to Ahab, Rachel regresses to adolescence within seconds. Her motor patterns coarsen; her telepathic broadcast widens and leaks; her tactical judgement narrows to kill-or-flee. She is, near him, the most dangerous and the most fragile she ever becomes. The risk is not that he will defeat her — it is that she will succeed in killing him in a way that confirms his curriculum.

*Leverage he holds.* Every restraint posture, the harpoon iconography, the word "Hound," and the conditioning phrases buried in her brainstem from adolescence. Also: knowledge of the children she was forced to bring in. He is the keeper of the names.

*Leverage she holds.* Total Omega-class power. She has, on paper, the capacity to unmake him at the molecular level. She does not. The non-act is itself the wound — every restraint is a confession that something in her still answers to him.

---

#### B. The Sentinels (Institutional Enemy, Class)

Not a person, but the file must treat them as such because her nervous system does. They are the apparatus that murdered her family and ran the camps. Sentinel silhouettes — including the Nimrod variants and the post-*Powers of X* iterations — produce reliable somatic responses across the record: pupillary dilation, telekinetic micro-flare (objects on her person tremble before she registers the cause), and immediate spatial-tactical recalculation. She has the most consistent, weaponized hatred toward Sentinels of any X-character in the canon, exceeding even Kate Pryde's, because for Kate the camps are a prevented hypothetical and for Rachel they are a lived past. She does not engage Sentinels — she *erases* them, with prejudice that approaches cosmic register. Failure mode: against a Nimrod-class unit she will burn resources she cannot afford rather than disengage.

---

#### C. Selene (Selene Gallio)

*History.* The Black Queen, the millennia-old psychic vampire. The first being to nearly kill Rachel in present-time canon (*UXM* #207), gutting her on a blade during the confrontation in which Rachel donned the Phoenix costume publicly.

*Dynamic.* Mirror-antagonist. Selene is what Rachel fears Phoenix-Rachel could become: ancient, sustained on the life-force of others, unmoored from kin, fully integrated with predation. The fight in #207 is not incidentally cosmic — it is a contest of self-images. Rachel pulls back from a killing blow at the last instant; Selene reads the hesitation as weakness; the wound she gives Rachel is an instructional cut. *This is what mercy costs you.*

*Trust/safety.* Predator/prey clarity. There is something almost restful in the relationship — Selene is unambiguous in a way most of Rachel's relationships are not.

*Failure mode.* Rachel cannot fight Selene without the Phoenix flaring beyond intended bounds. Selene is metabolically interesting to the Phoenix in a way that compromises Rachel's veto.

*Leverage.* Selene knows — and has named — Rachel's hunger to consume in order to feel whole. That naming is its own injury.

---

#### D. Mojo / The Mojoverse Apparatus

*History.* Excalibur era and onward; spaghetti-western abductions, gladiator arenas, *Mojoworld* sequences in which Rachel and Betsy Braddock are repeatedly captured, costumed, and made to perform.

*Dynamic.* Mojo is the *televisual* version of Ahab — another regime that owns her image, dresses her body, scripts her actions, and consumes her suffering as content. Where Ahab is the trauma of forced labor, Mojo is the trauma of forced performance. She loathes him with a specific, theatrical revulsion that is distinct from her Sentinel-hatred: it is the loathing of someone who has been made into a costume.

*Failure mode.* In Mojoverse environments she becomes erratic about who is and is not a "real" agent — a derealization response, plausibly trauma-derived. She has, on more than one occasion, treated Mojo-spawned simulacra as people and people as simulacra.

*Leverage Mojo holds.* The Hound brand reads, on Mojo-television, as *brand identity*. He has marketed her scars. Few injuries are deeper.

---

#### E. The Shi'ar Death Commandos & Vulcan-era Shi'ar Imperium

*History.* The Death Commandos, dispatched to exterminate the bloodline of the Phoenix host, murder Rachel's maternal grandparents on Earth (*UXM* vol.1 #466, 2006). She tracks them across the galaxy through the *Rise & Fall of the Shi'ar Empire* arc and into *Emperor Vulcan*.

*Dynamic.* This is the arc where Rachel's hunter is given a *just* target and the file gets to observe what she looks like when she is not internally divided about killing. The answer is sobering: she is methodical, patient, and uses Ahab's tracking discipline without conflict. She executes Commandos. She does not feel cleansed afterward. The episode is a clean disproof of the fantasy that righteous violence would heal her.

*Vulcan* — her own uncle, a Summers — is a separate and more complicated antagonist, who blurs into the family-of-origin file but bears mention here: he is the proof that "Summers blood" is not innocence. Encountering him destabilized a foundational comfort.

*Failure mode.* She came home from the Shi'ar campaign quieter, and the quietness took years to leave.

---

#### F. Sabretooth (Victor Creed)

*History.* The Mutant Massacre, *UXM* #213. He nearly kills her in the Morlock tunnels; she is found bleeding into the dark.

*Dynamic.* Sabretooth is a predator in the register she was trained to *be*. Encountering him is encountering an undisciplined version of her own conditioning, and the encounter cost her blood she could not afford to lose given her concurrent Phoenix-strain and Selene-injury. She does not have a sustained vendetta with him the way Logan does; what she has is a recognition, and the recognition is more unsettling than enmity. He sees her too — the file should note that of all her enemies, he is the one who has occasionally *spoken to her about hunting*, treating her as a colleague. The horror of being claimed as kin by a man like that is its own ongoing wound.

*Leverage.* He knows what she smells like when she is afraid.

---

#### G. The Phoenix Force (as Antagonist-Mode)

The file must record Phoenix in both this section and Section 8 (Non-Human Bonds), because the Force functions as enemy, lover, mother, and self by turns. Treated here in its antagonistic mode: Phoenix is the entity that has, repeatedly, threatened to dissolve Rachel into itself. It is the only adversary against which her standard tactical doctrine — overwhelming psionic force — is inverted: she must *withhold* power, not deploy it, and withholding is the discipline she was never taught. Her relationship to Phoenix-as-enemy is the closest thing in the canon to an addiction file: craving, use, remorse, abstinence, relapse, and finally — by the Prestige and *X-Men: Red* era — something resembling sober coexistence. The leverage Phoenix holds over her is total: it knows what she would be if she stopped grieving, and the offer is perpetually open.

---

### II. RIVALS — STRUCTURAL OPPOSITIONS, NOT ENMITIES

#### A. Emma Frost

*History.* Emma is the woman in Scott Summers's bed who is not Rachel's mother. The configuration alone constitutes a structural injury. Crystallized across the *Morrison-era*, *Astonishing*, and the Krakoan Quiet Council period.

*Dynamic.* This is the most psychologically rich rivalry in Rachel's record because it is not, on its face, an enmity. Emma has, on several occasions, been operationally protective of Rachel; she has used the title "stepmother" with deliberate provocation; she has trained junior telepaths with Rachel's tacit cooperation. But the relationship is built on a wound Emma cannot help embodying. Rachel grew up — in the future-she-came-from — knowing her mother as a particular woman; meeting the present-day Jean is already destabilizing; finding her father pair-bonded to *Emma specifically* is destabilizing twice over, because Emma is in some ways the negative image of Jean: cold where Jean is warm, performatively erotic where Jean is romantic, surgical where Jean is intuitive.

*Trust/safety.* Conditional and operational. Rachel will fight beside Emma without hesitation and will not eat at a table with her by choice. The telepathic etiquette between them is icy and immaculate — neither has ever, on the record, scanned the other casually. That mutual restraint is itself a form of acknowledgement.

*Failure mode.* Under stress, Rachel will weaponize the legitimacy wound ("you are not her mother") and Emma will weaponize the dispossession wound ("you are not from this world") and both will draw blood without either growing.

*Leverage.* Emma has stood in the Cerebro chair Jean once stood in. That fact does not need to be spoken to operate.

---

#### B. Stryfe

*History.* Stryfe — the clone of Cable, raised by Apocalypse in the alternate Askani-adjacent future — is the corruption of the project Rachel devoted her death to. She raised Nathan to be a savior; Stryfe is the negative of that work made flesh. Encountered most directly in *X-Cutioner's Song* and later resurgences.

*Dynamic.* Maternal-rivalrous in the strangest configuration in the file. Stryfe is, functionally, her bad son. She did not raise him and yet she did — he is what her project produced as byproduct. She regards him with horror, pity, and a residual ownership she will not name aloud. When he speaks to her, she answers; this is more than she does for most enemies.

*Failure mode.* She cannot kill him cleanly. The hesitation is not Phoenix-related; it is parental.

---

#### C. Kate Pryde (as Rival)

Treated extensively in Relationships II as a primary peer-bond; noted here because the relationship has a structural rivalrous component the file should not elide. Kate is, in the original timeline, the woman who *replaced* Rachel as the hero of "Days of Future Past" — Kate's consciousness was sent back, not Rachel's, despite Rachel being the telepath who initiated the link. Kate got to alter history; Rachel got to stay in the burning future. The asymmetry has never been adjudicated in canon and Rachel has never raised it. The file infers that she has metabolized it as one more debt she chose to pay. The non-mention is itself the data.

---

#### D. Madelyne Pryor

*History.* The Goblin Queen, clone of Jean, mother of the Nathan who became Cable, ex-wife of the Scott who is Rachel's father. The configuration is geometrically impossible to navigate cleanly.

*Dynamic.* Madelyne is the woman who occupied Rachel's mother's body and bore the brother Rachel raised. To encounter her — *Inferno* (1989), and again in the 2021 *Trial of Magneto / Inferno* materials — is to encounter someone who is, in three different senses, kin, none of them metabolizable. Rachel's response across the record has been a wary, sorrowful refusal of either enmity or sisterhood. She does not fight Madelyne with full force; she does not embrace her; she keeps a precise distance that is, for Rachel, almost diplomatic.

*Leverage.* Madelyne can speak the words "your brother" with biological standing. That standing is unappealable.

---

#### E. The Sisterhood of Mutants / Hellfire-adjacent female antagonists

A class of rivals — Selene's revivified court, the Hellfire Cult, junior Black-Queen aspirants — that Rachel handles with a contemptuous economy. They want what she has been offered and refused; they want the title and the dress and the throne. She cannot take them seriously as persons. Failure mode: she underestimates them because they bore her.

---

### III. LOVERS & INTIMATE BONDS

The file treats this category with particular care because the subject's intimate record is *sparse, late-blooming, and structurally idiosyncratic*. Rachel's erotic life has been editorially shy and developmentally consistent — a person who could not safely be touched in adolescence does not arrive at adult intimacy on a normative timeline. The pattern below is what the canon supports.

#### A. Franklin Richards (Adolescent Imprint, *Excalibur* / *Days of Future Present* era)

*History.* The bond with Franklin Richards is the earliest documented attachment in the romantic-adjacent register. In *Days of Future Present*, the future-adult Franklin and Rachel share a love that, in our present timeline, can never be consummated because the Franklin in question is a child here. The configuration is precisely the kind of temporal Möbius-strip Rachel inhabits comfortably and almost no one else can.

*Dynamic.* First love, accessible only across time. The relationship is real and unreciprocable, which suits Rachel's defensive architecture — it lets her practice the affect of love at a safe distance.

*Trust/safety.* High in the safest possible way: he was never going to be available, so he never had to fail her.

*Evolution.* She has carried him as a tender filed-away truth for decades; she does not pursue him in present-time, and she treats child-Franklin with a gentleness that is the relationship's only legible footprint.

*Failure mode.* The bond confirmed her template that love arrives from another timeline and stays there. The template is not healthy.

---

#### B. Korvus Rook'shir (The Adult Choice — *Uncanny X-Men* vol.1 #475 onward)

*History.* Shi'ar warrior, wielder of the Blade of the Phoenix, met during the *Rise & Fall of the Shi'ar Empire* arc. He is the only person in the published record with whom Rachel sustains a recognizably adult sexual-romantic partnership.

*Dynamic.* This is the relationship the file rests its prognosis on. Korvus does not require Rachel to perform normality, does not flinch at the brand, and shares the Phoenix-frequency at a register that lets her be psionically open without disintegrating the partner. He met her at her hunter-self — during the Commando arc — and was not frightened. The first sustained touch she permitted as an adult was his.

*Trust/safety.* The highest of any intimate bond on record. He is the partner with whom her startle response is lowest and her telepathic shielding is most relaxed. She sleeps near him. The file notes this because she does not, generally, sleep near anyone.

*Evolution.* Sustained across the Shi'ar arcs and *X-Men: Legacy*; geographically interrupted (he remains in Shi'ar space when she returns Earthside); never formally ended. The relationship has the structure of an indefinite continuance rather than a closure.

*Failure mode.* Geography and species-politics. Also: she did not, when Earth-events demanded, choose to stay with him. The choice was correct and it was also a wound; she has not unmade it.

*Leverage.* He has seen the Phoenix in her at close range and is still alive. The trust this generated is structurally irreplaceable.

---

#### C. Nightcrawler (Adjacent Affection — *Excalibur*, ambient)

*History.* Across the original *Excalibur* run, the file logs a quiet, sustained mutual fondness between Rachel and Kurt Wagner that never resolves into romance but bears noting. He treats her with a courtly, religious tenderness; she relaxes in his presence as she does with few males.

*Dynamic.* Probably best classed as a deep aesthetic-spiritual friendship with an erotic shimmer neither party converted. Kurt's Catholicism and Rachel's Askani mysticism share a vocabulary of grace; she is, with him, occasionally *theological*, which she is with almost no one else.

*Trust/safety.* High, non-sexual, undemanding. He is one of the few men in her record whose hand she will take without warning him first.

*Failure mode.* None operative; the relationship's stability derives from its un-conversion. Forcing it into romance would likely have destroyed it.

---

#### D. Betsy Braddock (Charged Sisterhood — *Excalibur* era and after)

The file records this as adjacent to intimate because the *Excalibur* run develops a charged, body-aware closeness between Rachel and Betsy that does not become sexual on the page but is more physically intimate than her bonds with most men. Shared captivity in the Mojoverse, shared psionic frequencies, shared costuming. They have been inside each other's minds. The bond is sisterly with an undercurrent of erotic recognition that neither names — a configuration the file flags as the most likely candidate for a future intimate development that the canon has not yet executed.

---

#### E. The Phoenix Force (as Intimate)

Listed here in its intimate-mode as the file promised. The bonding sequences across *UXM* #199–209, the Mother Askani period, and *X-Men: Red* read, behaviorally, as a long-form erotic-mystical attachment: invitation, surrender, ecstasy, regret, withdrawal, reunion. No human partner has ever produced the affective signature she produces in full Phoenix-union. The file must therefore acknowledge that her most intense lifelong intimacy is with a cosmic entity that is also her family inheritance, her enemy, and her possible undoing. Korvus is, in this light, the only partner who could be admitted to her bed because he met her on this frequency without being consumed by it.

*Trust/safety.* The most volatile of all intimate bonds; the one she has worked longest to render safe; the only one she has successfully negotiated from possession toward partnership.

*Failure mode.* Relapse. There is no terminal cure.

*Leverage.* Mutual and total.

---

### IV. CROSS-CATEGORICAL OBSERVATIONS

Several through-patterns the file underscores:

**Pattern 1 — The enemies are clearer than the lovers.** A trauma-survivor signature. She has been organized around threat for so long that adversarial relationships are legible and intimate ones are improvisational. The asymmetry of this section's length is not editorial bias; it is the subject.

**Pattern 2 — Hunter recognizes hunter.** Her most psychologically charged enemies (Ahab, Sabretooth, Selene, Stryfe) all share a predatory register with her own conditioning. She has no equivalently charged enmity with a non-predator antagonist. This confirms the Section 3 thesis: her aggressor-identification is structural, not incidental.

**Pattern 3 — The intimate partners she has sustained are all, in some register, off-world or out-of-time.** Franklin from another timeline; Korvus from another galaxy; Phoenix from another order of being. The pattern suggests an unconscious geometry in which intimacy is tolerable only at a distance that prevents the partner from witnessing her daily self. The file flags this as the next frontier of her recovery — whether she can, in the late integrated period, accept a partner who lives in the same room she does.

**Pattern 4 — Mercy is her recurring tactical liability and her recurring moral signature.** Every major failure mode in this section reduces to: she did not finish a kill she could have finished. The file declines to recommend correction. The mercy is the wall between her and the curriculum Ahab built.

**Pattern 5 — She has never, in the published record, taken a lover from among her enemies, nor an enemy from among her lovers.** The categorical hygiene is unusual for someone with her psionic intimacy-access and her trauma profile. The file infers this is deliberate — one of her organizing rules of the self, possibly unconscious. She does not mix the registers. The brand on her face was made by someone who owned her; she will not give that grammar of ownership to another partner, ever.

---

*End Section 7. Continued in Section 8: Non-Human Bonds (Phoenix Force, Timestream, Krakoan Resurrection Protocols, Cerebro-Sentience Interfaces).*


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU DOSSIER — RACHEL ANNE SUMMERS
## SECTION 6: POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

---

### 6.0 Preamble: Reading the Power as Symptom

Before cataloguing what Rachel Summers *can do*, the file must register the analytical premise that organizes the catalogue: **with this subject, the powers are not separable from the pathology.** Other Omega-class telepaths have abilities that exist in parallel to their psychology — Charles Xavier's telepathy is, in practice, an instrument he wields. Jean Grey's telepathy is, in practice, an instrument she wields. Rachel's telepathy is, in practice, **the form her nervous system takes**. Her scanning is hypervigilance; her telekinetic discharges are panic; her precognition is anticipatory grief; her Phoenix-bonds are dissociative state-shifts that happen to be cosmically powered. To map the powers without mapping the somatics underneath is to misunderstand the file. This section therefore catalogues capacity and signature-of-use together.

---

### 6.1 Telepathy

**Class.** Omega. Ceiling-level on the published scale, comparable in raw register to Jean Grey, Charles Xavier, the Cuckoos in concert, Emma Frost at organic peak, and Nate Grey. Distinct in *texture* from each.

**Mechanism, as evidenced.** Rachel's telepathy operates by what reads in-panel as a **fanned, sweeping awareness** rather than a single-target probe. Where Xavier's signature is the surgical insertion and Frost's is the diamond-edged cut, Rachel's is the **wide net pulled tight** — she perceives crowds and rooms as a single weave of minds and locates anomalies by pattern rather than by address. This is almost certainly a Hound-trained habit. The Hound program needed trackers who could sift a population for mutant signatures at distance; Rachel learned to *fish* before she ever learned to converse mind-to-mind, and the technique imprinted on her base methodology.

**Sub-capacities documented in canon:**

- **Telepathic communication and link-formation.** Bidirectional; she initiates the foundational link of the entire "Days of Future Past" arc (*UXM* #141), reaching across two decades and a timeline boundary to find Kate Pryde. This is, on the merits, one of the largest single telepathic feats in the record — the link held a consciousness-transfer across temporal distance, which is generally agreed in-universe to require both telepathic and chronal substrate.
- **Mind-reading, surface to deep.** She can extract surface thought without subject awareness; deep extraction requires either consent or override and leaves her exhausted (consistent across *Excalibur* and *X-Men: Gold*).
- **Mental shielding (own and others').** She maintains layered shields by default; their texture has shifted across the publication — early-era shields are spiky, defensive, easily detected by other psis as a "porcupine" signature, while late-era Prestige shields read as smoother and more concealing.
- **Mind control and suggestion.** Capable but used sparingly and with visible moral hesitation. The Hound program required her to *compel* targets toward capture; she retains the technique and refuses, by stated policy, to deploy it except under extremity.
- **Memory editing.** Documented but rarer in her hand than in Xavier's or Frost's. She has done it (cleanups after public mutant incidents) and dislikes it; the analytical reading is that *editing memory is what was done to her*, and she will not casually replicate the violation.
- **Psionic combat, mental-plane projection, astral form.** Standard at her tier. Notably, her astral form often retains the Hound tattoo — a tell that her self-image has not separated from the brand even in pure-mind space. (This is a diagnostic datum: see §3.)
- **Translation/language acquisition.** Demonstrated; the standard top-tier telepath utility.
- **Sensing mutant signatures at distance.** This is the **Cerebro-without-Cerebro** capacity, and it is unusually developed in Rachel because **it is the original Hound application**. She does not need the device. She *is* the device, refit for benign use. Operationally she can sweep a city; with concentration, a region; with strain and risk, a hemisphere.
- **Telepathic illusion / consensual reality manipulation.** Demonstrated in Mojoworld arcs and in confrontations with the Shi'ar — she can convince a target's nervous system of stimuli that are not occurring, including pain stimuli with somatic effect.

**Signature applications.**

- **The "hunter sweep."** When she enters a new environment, her unconscious default is a fanned low-level scan for hostile intent and for mutant signatures within roughly a block's radius. She does not turn this off. It is not a chosen tactic; it is a posture, the psionic equivalent of a Vietnam veteran's seat-back-to-the-wall. This is the clearest case in the file of an ability that has fused with a symptom.
- **Trauma reading by tattoo-recognition.** She is unusually skilled at detecting *other* survivors of conditioning, restraint, or psionic abuse, because she knows the shape of those scars in mind-space. Documented across her interactions with Generation X kids, the Morlock survivors, and later Krakoan returnees.
- **Family-recognition seeking.** A persistent, slightly involuntary subroutine: when in proximity to Scott, Jean, Nathan, or any genetic adjacency thereto, her telepathy *leans toward them*. She is monitoring their well-being constantly. Both parents have, in the record, noticed and commented gently on this.

**Limits.**

- **Cerebral fatigue.** Sustained high-bandwidth use produces nosebleeds, ocular hemorrhage, migraine. (Pattern visible from *UXM* #207 onward and reiterated in *X-Men: Red*.)
- **Conditioned aversion to compulsion.** She *can* mind-control; she will not in most cases. This is a moral limit, not a capacity limit, but the file should treat it as functionally hard because she is exceptionally consistent.
- **Permeability under restraint.** Historically, metal at the throat (collar-trigger) degrades her shielding. Late-record evidence suggests this has improved with therapeutic exposure but not extinguished.
- **Phoenix-aliasing.** When the Phoenix-force is in proximity or actively bonded, her telepathy bleeds into cosmic-register, which costs her surgical control. She becomes *louder* and less precise; subordinate minds in the area report headache, déjà vu, and overheard fragments of her thought. She does not always notice she is broadcasting.

---

### 6.2 Telekinesis

**Class.** Omega. Equal-rank to her telepathy; the two are not "primary/secondary" in her case the way they often are in Jean's earlier presentations. Rachel is a true dual-discipline Omega.

**Mechanism.** Force-projection along lines of will. Macro and micro scales both demonstrated: she has stopped vehicles in motion, ripped Sentinel hulls open, redirected energy beams, and — at the other end — performed fine-motor work, including stitched-suture-grade tissue manipulation (Askani-era field medicine) and, in *Excalibur*, the disassembly and reassembly of small mechanical devices without touching them.

**Sub-capacities:**

- **Flight.** Standard Omega-TK self-lift. Posture in flight is characteristic — arms slightly out from sides, head down, not the heraldic-Christ pose Jean adopts. She flies like someone who learned by fleeing.
